1. Xmas FMC 2006
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 08 Jan 2007 09:16:55 -0000

Hey all!

Since Dan is away all this week i would like to post my "results"
here.
For now i will only submit the lenghts of my found solutions. As i
understand it Dan will not accept entries for this challenge already
since a new one is already open. I will submit my full solutions here
if there is any interest:

2x2x2: 5 turns
2x2x3: 8 turns
1 roux: 5 turns
2 roux: 12 turns
f2l: 14 turns
edges only: 12 turns (no useful corners gained)
1-face/1-layer: 9 turns (same solution, didn't find shorter for just
1-face than for 1-layer, a bit odd, but found many of same length;-))
full cube: 23 turns(10 + 7 + 9 + 1 - 4)
corners only: no time to complete my search for this, when full cube
was done it was already 11 pm day before the deadline and i was
knackered.

I found all these solutions during the saturday, 7-8 hrs spent in
total ;-)

Have fun!

-Per

4. Alternative "metric" for fewest moves!
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 24 Jan 2007 10:23:19 -0000

I'm forwarding a message i got from Richard Carr:

(Personally, I think a better way to measure this fewest moves record
is either a) least
number of moves taken above the optimum or least ratio (moves
taken)/optimum, After
all, if non-optimal solution 19 was reached (for a cube requiring 18
moves( then a solution
requiring 20 moves could never even equal the record even if the
optimal solution was
found.

This sounds to me like a great idea. Especially for official world
records. Of course it will not change results order each week for the
fmc. And if fmc starts to use this, the "categories" must be changed
also. For instance a ratio better than 1.5 is expert, better than 2.0
is advanced and so on ...

Rating system may also change. Instead of using the average solution
length in the calculations one could use the optimal length
multiplied by a factor...

Anyone has any comments??

-Per

6. 60 or 90 minutes?
From: Lars Petrus <lars@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 28 Jan 2007 13:51:45 -0800

I've been talking to the WCA guys about the timing for official FMC
competitions.

I think 60 minutes is just too little time to do a serious FMC
solution. With all the time spent setting up your cubes, getting
started on on side, and writing down the solution and double checking
it on the other, I'm pretty much just using 30 minutes for actual
solving. My suggestion is to use 90 minutes instead, which would
effectively double the time, while still not killing the schedule for
a one day competition.

An other argument is that Mirek's official world record was in fact
set using 90 minutes, which makes things fairly odd right now.

They're not opposed to the idea, but they want to hear this from a
majority of FMC solvers, rather than just me. Which is as it should be.

I realize some people would prefer to have much shorter fewest moves
competitions where you get to solve in a few minutes, just counting
actual physical moves. I think that is a very valid competition
format that I would really like to do, but I think it's a *very*
different thing than the FMC solving, and those things should be
separate events with separate rules , records, titles etc. Trying to
force both things into some compromise would just combine the worst
of both worlds.

So what do people think?

- - - - - - - - - - - -
"The mind is like a parachute. It doesn't work unless it's open."
--- Frank Zappa

Lars Petrus - lars@... http://lar5.com

10. Re: RE: [fewestmoveschallenge] 60 or 90 minutes?
From: "Ron van Bruchem" <ron@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Mon, 29 Jan 2007 17:08:25 +0100

Hi guys,

I saw this discussion and thought: "Oh no, not again.... :-(".

What is the goal of the event?
I think it is to decide which competitor is best at solving FMC, given certain parameters.
So the goal is not to find the shortest solution ever for FMC.

An important parameter is the time for the solve.
I think the optimal time for the solve is the time:
1) needed for competitors to find a decent solution and check it
2) suitable for competition organisers to fit the FMC event into the time schedule (FMC will often be overlapping with other events)
3) appealing enough for non frequent FMC competitors to also give it a try (only a little overlapping is OK)

I see no proof that 1) is a problem.
I know 2) is a problem. There were actually competitors who wanted to do 'best of 2' or 'average of 2' because that would be a better format.
I see that 3) the number of competitors has risen since FMC is 60 minutes.

12. Re: 60 or 90 minutes?
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 29 Jan 2007 23:12:31 -0000


Hi Lars,

> They're not opposed to the idea, but they want to hear this from a
> majority of FMC solvers, rather than just me. Which is as it should be.

I supposed the WCA guys you talked too are Tyson. When he submitted
the request, I gave my opinion which was:

"60 minutes is not that short. Of course you can find better solutions
with more time, but:

- Because you need time for scrambling, solving, checking, writing, 30
mins is a bit too short.
- In 60 mins, you got time to find advanced solutions.
- In 90 mins, you could do better, but maybe not significantly better.
- 3 hours or more: that's what some FMCers would need..."

So I agree that 60 minutes is the minimum for such an event that is
very different from linear cubing (fast or slow).

Per's comment seems to confirm my last point, and I think many would
still be very angry with "only" 90 minutes, but I can perfectly
understand that with 90 minutes some can feel much more comfortable
and get more elaborate intuitions and solutions.

How many cubers do consider that 60 minutes is hardly acceptable,
whereas 90 minutes would be much better?

Gilles.


P.S.: I used to naïvely consider speed solving and optimized solving
as equally important. So, I was a bit sad when we had only 1 round (1
hour) for the whole optimized solving category at the european champ,
because of lack of popularity and organizational constraints.

21. FMC companion configuration
From: "fonnny" <superdoubledan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 19 Feb 2007 14:30:47 -0000

First of all, let me just say that I love the FMC companion - it's quite helpful for me. One thing I did find somewhat annoying is having to keep changing the colors of the (online) cube to match my physical cube. It's nice that the option is there to change them, but it was frustrating not having them persist. So in looking at the HTML code, I found a way to set it up the way my cube is, and I thought I'd share it in case anyone else could be helped by this. You don't have to be HTML savvy to do this, but it certainly helps. There are 2 things that need to change. First, in right.html find the line that says <param name="faces" value="r,g,w,o,b,y"> That's the colors for the front, right, down, back, left and up faces. Change those letters to the colors of your cube. In my case, I changed it to say <param name="faces" value="b,o,y,g,r,w"> - white on top, blue in front and red to the left Then, in left.html look for the line that says <td><select name="front"><option value="b">Blue</option><option value="g">Green</option><option value="o">Orange</option><option value="r" selected>Red</option><option value="w">White</option><option value="y">Yellow</option></select></td> That's the select box for the front face. Currently red is "selected" by having the keyword selected right after the value tag. Move the word selected to after the color that is your front face (in my case blue). So mine says <td><select name="front"><option value="b" selected>Blue</option><option value="g">Green</option><option value="o">Orange</option><option value="r" >Red</option><option value="w">White</option><option value="y">Yellow</option></select></td> You'll have to do that for each of the 6 lines corresponding to the 6 faces. Hope this helps someone. I was wondering where all the other solvers were for FMC 141 - when I looked at the results yesterday I was very surprised to see myself having (apparently) won?!? I see now the full results are up and I find myself in 5th - much closer to where I would expect to be :-) dan.

23. FMC#142 - cubestation trouble
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 25 Feb 2007 13:32:08 -0000

Hi guys :-)

I wanted to submit my solution to fmc #142 yesterday, but dans site
was already mistakenly showing #143 already. So, because of that i
had to email Dan my solution. It seems he is not around this
weekend, probably at Belgian Open so i just gonna show my solution
here:

Solution:
B' L U' R' B2 D2 L U R F' D' R2 D R L' F2 L B' L' F2 L R B' R' B R'

Quote:
B' L U' R' B2 D2 L U R F' does a 2x2x3 block. Now i do D' R2 D
leaving 4 edges and 2 corners to be solved. The following conjugate
solves the 4 edges and leaves 3 corners unsolved: R B' R-B'-R' B R'.
Then i have B' L U' R' B2 D2 L U R F' R.B' R B' R' B R' Now i insert
L' F2 L B' L' F2 L B at the dot cancelling 2 turns and gaining a
slice turn :-) About 2 hours spent in total. Still im too slow ...

Best wishes,

Per

26. Re: Belgium FMC
From: "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 26 Feb 2007 09:54:53 -0000

Hi Gilles =) Here is my solution: QR' - R' F' L F2 U2 L2 F - U' B2 U2 R2 - U R' U F' U2 F R - L U2 L' U' L U2 L' - U' F' U' F U R' F R F' - R U R' U L' U R U' L U2 R' - U2 - L2 U S U2 S' U L2 56 HTM 53 STM 68 QTM (if I counted it correctly) At first a cube orintation QR', then a 2x2x2 block R' F' L F2 U2 L2 F, expand to Xcross U' B2 U2 R2, second pair U R' U F' U2 F R, third L U2 L' U' L U2 L', last pair U' F' U' F U R' F R F', and then a inverse Sune for CLL R U R' U L' U R U' L U2 R', align U-layer using U2 and then the only easy step I had in this solve, a U-PLL as the ELL case L2 U S U2 S' U L2 Not wery good, I seldomly get this hig figures, but it is at leat a result and I only spent 21 of the 60 minutes to get here. // Kenneth --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...> wrote: > > > The scramble was: > > R' F2 L2 U' B' R' B' F2 D L D2 F' D' L' B L2 B' F2 L U' R' D' U' L' F > R' B2 D' B' R > > You have 1 hour to beat 32. ;-) > > Gilles. >

36. RE: FW: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC 148
From: "Dan Harris" <dan_j_harris@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Fri, 6 Apr 2007 20:43:06 +0100

OK, here is what I do.



Imagine I have found a skeleton solution with 3 corners left to solve. These
could be any 3 corners, and twisted in any direction.



So I then put numbers on the pieces, 1, 2 and 3. You have to make sure that
you are doing the correct cycle of stickers, not just pieces. So if for
example, you put number 1 on the Yellow of Yellow-Green-Red, then number 2
would go on the corner piece that is in the Yellow-Green-Red position, upon
the sticker that would be Yellow if the Yellow-Green-Red were in it's
correct position.



Now, we can track those corner stickers at any point in the solution. The
three corners can be solved using an 8-move commutator, which you make up on
the fly. The commutator is of the form PQP'Q'. So when going through the
solution, after each move you note where the corners are, and try to find 8
move commutators which solve the 3-cycle. It doesn't matter where in the
solution you do it, because the commutator affects only those 3 corners, so
you try to do it at the optimal point, where the most moves cancel. You
would be lucky to cancel more than 4 moves I think, but 4 is very good.



On a solved cube, you could do R' D R U2 R' D' R U2 - this is one of those 8
move commutators, see how it affects the 3 corners only.



Im very rushed for time, so can't explain further, but if anyone else could
help clarify what I've written (if needed) that would be great.



All this was made possible by the Godfather of commutators in FMC and
cubing, Per Kristen Fredlund.



Dan :-)

Hi :-) 2 tips for now: When u end up with edge or corner 3-cycle put numbered stickers on them so that cycling them in order of the stickers will solve them. In some cases this may not be possible (if you have singular flipped edge(s) or twisted singular corners. Or other "bad" cycles. Understanding cycles is a main concept for understanding what goes on during the insertions. Another thing is making up 3-cycles from basic principles, namely commutators and conjugates. These are not hard concepts to grasp. But surely this is stepping outside normal speedsolving paths. Fewest moves solving is not a tabular exercise!! I will come back with more, but i hope this will trigger the small grey cells to some serious self-study ;-) Have fun!! -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "david_thielen2002" <mthielen68@...> wrote: > > My problem is with insertions. Ok, although I think you might have worded it more clearly, to sound less like "We have Per to thank for insertions", and more like "*I* have Per to thank for *teaching me* insertions". For example, There is another technique for solving in few moves with the aid of a computer, known as the two phase algorithm, which works by defining a group G1 = <U,D,R2,L2,F2,B2> which is a subset of the entire cube group G = <U,D,R,L,F,B>. The ingenious algorithm works as follows. In the first phase, the algorithm searches for a sequence that will transform the cube into G1. Then the second phase searches for a sequence using "only" the moves from G1 to transform the cube into G. All this was made possible by Josef Jelinek, the Godfather of computer solver designers. I think most people would want to clarify that Herbert Kociemba and Josef Jelinek both developed similar solving programs, Herbert's was called "two-phase algorithm", Josef's was called ACube, and Herbert invented his first. (Not exactly the same kind of scenario, but good enough to get the idea, I think). I hope this explains how I interpreted your statement :-) In any case, I'm glad you have clarified everything above. -- Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/
41. Re: FW: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC 148

Hi :-) it could be that Mirek did insertions before me, but i thought that up the same on my own also early-mid 80s and i had never heard of anyone doing that before. Ironically i first used that technique exclusively for making pretty patterns, while i was actually still only doing linear fewest moves solutions, with basic backtracking, only. There was no code or anything of the kind to give me randowm scrambles back then , so i never wrote down the scrambles or solutions, just counting the moves - taking averages of 10. And i then did the same for supersolving because i found it even more challenging. If my memory is correct my best ever linear solves back then was 24 turns for normal solving and 28 turns for supersolving. My technique was the main part cross-first and then clever c/e pair insertions and corner cycling finishing. About the 2-phase solution, neither Herbert Kociemba nor Josef Jelinek developed that. Mike Reid was the first to make software based on that method. But with all respect Herbert Kociemba was the first who made a really user friendly version of a 2-phase solver for the public masses. Cube Explorer has lots of features ACube lacks, basically because these features do require a graphical frontend. Acube does also have its own unique features lacking in Cube Explorer. So it's great to have both :-) -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, Ryan Heise <ryan@...> wrote: > > Dan Harris wrote: > > > Well, Per was the one who took the time to sit down and explain it to me > > several times through what was a pretty excruciating process. 42. Re: FW: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC 148
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 21:55:40 +1000

Per Kristen Fredlund wrote:

> it could be that Mirek did insertions before me, but i thought that
> up the same on my own also early-mid 80s and i had never heard of
> anyone doing that before.

I'm not disputing this, so rest easy :-) My purpose is just to let
people know (who didn't already know) that this technique was first
introduced to the FMC by Mirek Goljan more than a year before you joined
FMC, and when we credit someone by saying "all this was made possible by
...someone..." we should be careful so that people are aware that Mirek
did introduce it earlier.

> About the 2-phase solution, neither Herbert Kociemba nor Josef
> Jelinek developed that. Mike Reid was the first to make software
> based on that method.

Now, staying with my "credit police" hat on ...

This is not true. You say it like it's true, so some people might
believe you, but again I think we should be extremely careful when
assigning credit.

The path of influence was as follows:

Thistlethwaite (4 phase)
-> Kociemba (2 phase)
------> Reid (optimal)
------> Jelinek (partial)

These people are also respectful and particular about giving credit, and
you will find nowhere on Kociemba's page that he based is work on Mike
Reid's. However, you will find on Mike Reid's page that he based his
work on Kociemba's. Similarly, Josef Jelinek gives credit to both
Kociemba and Reid.

Let's not diminish Kociemba's contribution. He not only developed the 2
phase algorithm, but also developed new and efficient ways to represent
cube permutations and orientations that made large-scale searches
possible.

--
Ryan Heise
http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/



43. Re: FW: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC 148
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 14 Apr 2007 22:05:39 +1000

Ryan Heise wrote:

> The path of influence was as follows:
>
> Thistlethwaite (4 phase)
> -> Kociemba (2 phase)
> ------> Reid (optimal)
> ------> Jelinek (partial)

I should add to this that after Reid developed his famous "optimal"
solver, based on Kociemba's work, the Kociemba used Reid's ideas to add
an "optimal" feature to his own CubeExplorer program. So, there is a
path of influence as follows:

Kociemba (2 phase) [1] -> Reid (optimal) [2] -> Kociemba (optimal) [3]

Here are some citations:

[1] http://kociemba.org/math/imptwophase.htm
[2] http://www.math.ucf.edu/~reid/Rubik/optimal_solver.html
[3] http://kociemba.org/math/optimal.htm

50. Re: FW: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC 148
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 17 Apr 2007 09:34:03 -0000

Hi :) It's common to have precomputed sequences for the G1 phase. Search in G is done until some predetermined length and a match in G1 is sought by simple table lookup (pruning). The advantage of ACube (so far) over Cube Explorer is that ACube uses pruning also for a non fully specified cube, whereas Cube Explorer doesn't do this as of yet. -Per --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, Ryan Heise <ryan@...> wrote: > > Ryan Heise wrote: > > > There is another technique for solving in few moves with the aid of a > > computer, known as the two phase algorithm, which works by defining a > > group G1 = <U,D,R2,L2,F2,B2> which is a subset of the entire cube group > > G = <U,D,R,L,F,B>. The ingenious algorithm works as follows. In the > > first phase, the algorithm searches for a sequence that will transform > > the cube into G1. 51. Rules
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 18 Apr 2007 17:23:45 +1000

1. Am I allowed to figure out a solution using a computer algorithm by
writing out the computations manually on a piece of paper (without a
computer)?

2. Am I allowed to use a computer cube simulator that automatically
notates the solution as I perform it, supports easy backtracking (e.g.
backspace key), and keeps track of all the different branches you try?

I know there was a rules document in the past, but I can't find it now.

--
Ryan Heise
http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/



52. RE: [fewestmoveschallenge] Rules
From: "Dan Harris" <dan_j_harris@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Wed, 18 Apr 2007 09:06:07 +0100

Hi Ryan, The best rule of thumb is to not do anything you couldn't do in a competition. Therefore if you want to write out computations on a piece of paper, that is perfectly allowable. If you can't do all that in less than 60 minutes, you have to of course wonder if it's very good practice for competition FMC, but for the purposes of the weekly online FMC which is a week long, that is fine. In a competition you wouldn't have access to a computer so no, you are not allowed to use a simulator which helps you along the way. I don't have a problem with people using FMC companion, because although it does make things easier and you couldn't do this in a competition, it doesn't do anything you couldn't do yourself manually. Again you have to decide whether its good practice for competition FMC, it might be better to get very fast at doing the backtracking using a piece of paper, a pen, and a cube. Dan _____ From: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com [mailto:fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com] On Behalf Of Ryan Heise Sent: 18 April 2007 08:24 To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com Subject: [fewestmoveschallenge] Rules 1. Am I allowed to figure out a solution using a computer algorithm by writing out the computations manually on a piece of paper (without a computer)? 2. Am I allowed to use a computer cube simulator that automatically notates the solution as I perform it, supports easy backtracking (e.g. backspace key), and keeps track of all the different branches you try? I know there was a rules document in the past, but I can't find it now. -- Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheis <http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/> e.com/cube/

61. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Rules
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 19 Apr 2007 11:38:38 +1000

ct wrote:

> > 2. Am I allowed to use a computer cube simulator that automatically
> > notates the solution as I perform it, supports easy backtracking (e.g.
> > backspace key), and keeps track of all the different branches you try?
>
> Does you have such a tool??? :) Does it already exist or is it only in
> the planning stages? If it already exists, I'd have a look at it, but
> I don't think I'd use it regularly: I like using a real cube. I
> suppose i wouldn't have a problem with something similar to your speed
> cube simulator as long as it doesn't suggest moves, algs, etc. Only
> recording moves, branches, etc is ok with me.

Well, there are already some bits of code, but it would need a lot more
effort to display the branches nicely etc.

My thoughts were, (1) notating wastes half the time (which implies it
wastes part of our lives); (2) resetting the cube and re-applying the
scramble also takes up time; (3) counting the number of moves takes up
time.

--> Therefore: (4) FMC is too tedious and wastes part of our lives.

If a computer helped to automate the non-creative parts of the process,
we could reclaim a lot of time for our lives.

Anyway, I will not continue work on the program because these things
generally take a lot of effort, and in this case the program is not
allowed to be used here. Instead, I will think more about what I can do
for a linear FMC.

--
Ryan Heise
http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/

Anything more means that the playing > field is not even with some people able to spare more time than others. > > (Yes, people will say 10 minutes is too short, because there is not > enough time to do notation! ;-) Maybe you should restart your 5 minute FMC. I would definitely participate, even though I'd prefer 2 minutes. ;-) But 5 or 10 is fine. Cubers have different solving styles so it's impossible to say what time limit is the "best". The simulator you were talking about sounds perfect. It would make FMC much more comfortable, scrambling and writing down the moves are the main reasons why I'm not very interested in it. And it would also be useful for analyzing direct (speed)solves afterwards. Chess players replay their games later to find their own mistakes/weaknesses, often with a computer. From my experience it helps in cubing as well. -- Johannes Laire

72. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Linear solve in 22(24) moves
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 27 Apr 2007 21:26:51 +1000

Ryan Heise wrote:

> Well, I made one mistake, so it is 24 moves with 2 useless ones. But
> still very lucky:
>
> http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/record.cgi?db=single_fewestmoves&name=Ryan+Heise

By the way, this solve raises an interesting question about counting
moves. If I had done this solve 10 years ago, I might have counted it as
22 moves, and silently not counted the mistake/undo. But I think we need
to be more strict if we want to turn this into a proper event.

Above, I counted the RR' mistake as 2 moves (very expensive), but maybe
a more reasonable approach is to count such mistakes as 1 move. A
justification for this might be to view it as R4 (1 move) instead of RR'
(2 moves). Does it sound reasonable?

--
Ryan Heise
http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/



73. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Rules
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com

Johannes Laire wrote:

> Luck might be a too big factor for "best of 3", so I'd prefer average.

"Best of 3" does have the advantage of allowing you to stop after the
first solve and submit DNS for the rest if you don't have that much time
to spend each week.

But I think I agree, I just got another 23 move linear solve yesterday.
Who knows, it could happen quite often when taking all of the
competitors into account. If so, then the winner would really be as good
as random. Still, such extremely lucky solves will significantly bring
down the average. Another option yet again is to use the "median".

So far, I am happy with 5 minutes per solve, and need to decide on:

- Average of / Median of / Best of?
- 3 solves or 5 solves?

Ryan Heise
http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/

77. Modified cube companion.
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 08 May 2007 11:00:33 -0000

Hi :)

Modifying the fewest moves companion has been discussed before. I
have made my modification available at this address:

http://cubix.idi.ntnu.no/fmccomp23_per/fmccomp.html

I made this modification with Internet Explorer in mind. It doesn't
look so nice in other browsers. The main differences are:

- 2 cube applets to play with
- more compact GUI
- i moved the buttons closer to the scramble & solution areas
- colored background options in the color scheme selection area

As mentioned already not all of this works so nuicely in other
browsers than Internet Explorer. My version does work in
IE/Opera/Firefox browsers. For some reason Dan's cubestation site
only has version 2.1 for download. Not Charles Tsai's latest 2.3
version, on which my version is based :-)

-Per




78. Re: 10 move lastlayer algorithms
From: "Cyril Castella" <cyrilca@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 24 May 2007 10:00:35 -0000

I wondered about that, too... I counted on Helmstetter's page (see link below) and got 44 LL positions that you can solve optimally in 10 moves exactly. And say a few more in 6-9. It is not a bunch of algs but this does not include mirrors and inverses, which are quite tricky to spot when OLL and PLL are involved at the same time ! Helmstetter's page: http://www.ai.univ-paris8.fr/~bh/cube/solutions_tout.html Congrats Guus, if you happen to come by here ! Cyril --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "ct" <c_w_tsai@...> wrote: > > Hi Guus, > I was curious about what you said in your quote about learning all 10 move last layer algorithms. How many algorithms are there? And do you know all of them or a smaller subset, like edges oriented?

80. Re: 10 move lastlayer algorithms
From: "guusrs" <guusrs@...>

Hi Guys, I mean all 10-moves algs that only affect the last layer. Some algs solve the same position but they are worth memorizing because it probably cancels out some moves. 85. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Cyril Castella" <cyrilca@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 01 Jun 2007 09:43:08 -0000

If you want a show event, then copy speed-chess, with a chess-clock between the two participants: - both have say 10 minutes - participant 1 makes a move and hits the clock - participant 2 makes a move and hits the clock - ... When your clock reaches 0, you lose. If your opponent solves the cube before you, you lose... Anyway, for me such a format is *not* FMC anymore. I do not think I will ever compete in any official event anyway... For me the "essence" of FMC is to solve the cube in the fewest moves as I can. My brain against the cube. All the tricks I can think of (insertions, mix of methods, some algs, commutators, ...) to lower down my move count. An alternative to brute force speedsolving. It is not a show ;-) It may be argued that it is another debate, but for me the actual Rubik's magic competitions are only dexterity shows : you can actually win such a competition and not know how to solve the puzzle starting from a given scrambled state. For me, FMC has always been the alternative to dexterity competitions. In my opinion, Gilles' idea (or 5 min. linear FMC solving) is fine for the show, but not for the spirit of FMC... My 2 Swiss Francs cents ;-) Cyril > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Gilles Roux" > <grrroux@> wrote: > > > > > > Please read: > > > > > http://www.worldcubeassociation.org/forum/viewtopic.php? p=1409&sid=9c88d&#92; > > 365f4e1fedd18e18e966dc42110#1409 > > > > Remember Dan? We talked about it in Brussels. > > > > > > Gilles. > > >

87. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 01 Jun 2007 12:09:31 -0000

Hi :-) Let us not mix up FMC and Fewest Moves. FMC is Dan's beloved online weekly fewest moves challenge. Fewest moves in a real-life competion can never be FMC ;-) I sometimes abuse the term FMC myself so don't take it too seriously :D I guess mixing fewest moves and FMC is a bit like mixing case and algo. What is meant is understood even if not phrased formally correct :-o I would also like to see a more spectator friendly fewest moves format, but not one that would take very long or need excessive amount of judges. Also it should not replace the corrent format that honors the spirit of (iterative) solving. To me the whole idea of fewest move solving is to NOT do a typical linear solve, but to explore many options and tricks to come up with a short optimised solution. Even though linear fewest moves solving with a short time limit may attract more participants and more spectators it will be a sellout for me, unless it's a supplement to the current 1 hr format. Ideally we should have 2 tries á 1 hr like for EC 2004. Whether to go for best of 2 or average of 2 is another discussion :D For me speedsolving shows off ONE aspect of cubing and fewest an entirely other. IMHO fewest moves is underrated amongst most cubers and seen as something somewhat esoteric. To me solving like that allows to show off a deeper understanding of the cube. And even not spectator friendly is worth something in itself. If speeeding can have many events like normal, OH and BLD why shouldn't fewest moves be able to have more than 1 event? For practical reasons i do not see how we could have fewest moves events for larger cubes. Also writing down long sequences is prone to errors. Normally during a 1 hr fewest moves event i do at least 1 silly mistake in notation that i waste a lot of time to track down! 92. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 02 Jun 2007 17:35:04 -0000

ct wrote:
>
> > For me, linear FMC is about solving and nonlinear FMC is about
> > searching.
>
>
> No, I think there's more to it than just simply searching.

Of course, but my point is that searching is a HUUUUUUGE part of
nonlinear FMC.

> For
> example, many of us could search forever and we still would not find
> the same solutions that Per does. I'm sure it's not because he
> searches longer than us, but because he has some understanding of
> the cube - maybe it's something subtle - that the rest of us don't
> quite have.

Sorry to burst this bubble, but there is not really much mystery to
the FMC techniques, I don't think.

Per's advanced method is designed to "create" search alternatives. He
allows for two insertion points instead of just one, and this means
that he creates many more combinations and this maximises his chances
of finding the lucky short solution. For example, suppose your
skeleton is 22 moves and you have one insertion left. There are 23
possible insertion points, and therefore you have increased your
chances of finding a good solution by 23 times. However, suppose your
skeleton is 16 moves and you have two insertions left. There are now
17 chances for the first insertion, and 17+N chances for the second
(where N is the length of the first inserted sequence) giving you the
multiplication of 17*(17+N) times increased chances to find a lucky case.

In other words, Per has a method that is well-suited to searching, and
as we know, Per has been open about the fact that he is willing to put
in enormous amounts of time to search these possibilities.

Now, I have to admit that this must be a valid sport. Of course, my
personal opinion is WHY???!?! Why would anyone be interested in
searching for so long? But you might also ask the question, WHY would
anyone care about doing the magic when it's the same sequence of hand
movements each time you perform it? Or, why are people interested in
speedsolving using a memorised system? I wonder that one myself. But
different people are interested in these things, and that makes them
valid.

My personal choice, however, is that I don't like this "try all
combinations" aspect to FMC. That is exactly what you have to do when
you look for an insertion point. You can't possibly know ahead of time
which is the best insertion point - you MUST test EVERY SINGLE POINT.
That is brute force, and in the end, what is this really testing?

I am more interested to see how well people can perform when they
aren't allowed second chances. I think this is a much better test of
solving skill, and one's understanding of the cube.

> I guess I'm a fewest moves purist: I would rather see fewest moves
> remain not understood than have more people interested in a
> "diluted" version :)

The fundamental skill in both styles is "linear solving". Once you
have that, you can dilute it by adding nonlinear techniques ;-)

But to be serious, many of us have always done fewest moves the linear
way. How can that be considered a diluted version?

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




93. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 03 Jun 2007 03:06:24 -0000

ct wrote:

> I consider (and I'm sure most people will agree) finding the
> skeleton the main task and by far, the harder task. Doing the
> insertion(s) is the lesser secondary task, just finishing it off.

I don't disagree with you.

My claim is that what nonlinear FMC adds over and above linear FMC is
the ability to search and test different paths, and then jump back in
time if it turned out to be the wrong way forward. Yes, it has linear
solving at its root, but one linear solve to test one path may take
only a few minutes, but a whole nonlinear FMC experience may take
several hours of searching, so "searching" is the word I used to
characterise nonlinear FMC because that is the reason why the process
takes so long.

Searching permeates every facet of nonlinear FMC. Not only at the
insertion stage, where brute force is used to search different
possible insertion points, but also during the construction of the
skeleton, since none of us here are capable of finding an expert-grade
skeleton on our first try, without a lot of L-U-C-K, and will
therefore just have a first try, and if it turns out bad, have a
second try, and so on.

For me, searching is an interesting application of computers, but it
is not something that I find interesting to do by hand. As a
programmer, if I ever have a hundred possibilities to test, I will
write a computer program to test them because Humans should never have
to suffer by performing such routine work. I am not saying it is an
invalid practice, but it is just not interesting to me :-)

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




94. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 03 Jun 2007 03:44:05 -0000

Lars Petrus wrote:

> That could be fun. You'd need to write down the made moves somehow,
> since a speedcuber can make several moves with one movement, as well
> as when no one is looking.

When I was thinking about linear FM solving as an event, I thought of
the following approach to judging, which my apply here, too:

The idea is for each competitor to "dictate" each move they make to
the judge as they solve. So, as you solve, you say out aloud "Right -
Up 2 - Right inverse - etc.". Then the judge replicates these moves on
their own cubes to verify that the competitor did actually perform
what he/she said and did not insert any extra moves while the judge
was not looking. The competitor can also watch as the judge replicates
each move to make sure that he/she has heard you correctly.

It could take some getting used to for each competitor to
automatically know that "Red side" translates to "Right". The
alternative is for the competitor to dictate the colour names, but
this would mean that the judge would need the same colour scheme as
the competitor which may not be feasible.

Another issue for this newly proposed event is that competitors should
not be able to see each other's cube, otherwise one cubist may copy
the other's moves. Yes, you could require the second competitor not to
use the same moves, but there is really no need for either competitor
to see each other's cube or even interact.

I like the idea of having 10 competitors up on stage side by side,
competing simultaneously. In a race, competitors also do not
"interact", they are racing individually but just trying to be faster
than everyone else. For speed cubing, it would be exciting, and easy
to see who finished first (and last :-). For fewest moves solving, we
can still have a race format even though we are measuring number of
moves rather than time - all we need to do is synchronise the moves
with some time interval - say 10 seconds per move.

If we can rig the timer devices, it might be possible to run the race
as follows:

- Every 10 seconds, there is a "beep" indicating that you may now lift
your hands from the pads and apply one move to the cube, and then
return your hands back to the pad.
- If you do not return your hands back to the pad before 10 seconds is
up, you will hear a "bzzzt" and you will receive a DNF result, or if
your cube is solved, then you will receive a move count.

> A lot of people would have a hard time doing their final algorithms
> one move at a time.

That sounds like a good feature :-)

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




95. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 03 Jun 2007 12:07:03 -0000

Hi Ryan!! Indeed you do make it sound like taking more time in fewest moves is only to allow for more excessive random searches. You are far from correct in this. Sorry!! Or at least it's only half the story. The point in spending more time is that one should have many targets for the search. That requires knowledge about what is a good "skeletal solution". And yes insertions is perhaps the key aspect of this. In addition to some other standard tricks like premoves/pseudoblocks. A thorough knowledge of commutators and conjugates is also important alongside more general short sequence construction knowledge. This may sound very vague. But it does involve things like utilising cyclical shifts and symmetries amongst other things. I'm actually a bit tired of hearing that when taking longer time to solve it just depends on luck. Of course it does depend on luck to some extent, but only partially. To be honest with you, a linear solve depends more on luck than a "search solve". That's a fact! Take this example: we have a linear solution competition with only 5 mins allocated. Let's say the scramble is not that hard and that the first 5/6 turns are rather obvious block making turns. 2 participants both see this good start. Now there would be a few good paths to choose from to carry on ... Choosing a path here with very limited time depends almost SOLELY on luck i'd say. I just had to say something about the luck aspect of fewest moves solving. It's often very misunderstood. When i started with fewest moves solving back in mid 80s i did solely linear solving because i never even wrote down the scramble. I had ONE attempt only. No way to set up the same scramble again. The only option to carry on from same scramble if i did mistake was to undo turns i had made. I quickly realised that i got some very good solves and some very bad ones this way. This is by and large due to the randomness of luck when sticking to ONE main path only. Taking averages of linear solving does make sense, but single ones not really. Too much luck ;-) If one takes more time and does iterative solving with all the tricks of the trade, one can combine a "linear average" into a "singular iterative" solve so to speak and avoid many of the luck aspects of single fast linear solves. Of course it's true that practising either linear or iterative solving you will gradually improve. The luck factor does decrease over time with practice. But IMHO linear solving involves much more luck than the current 1 hr official format. We can sacrifice that for making fewest moves more spectator friendly, i don't have problems with that. But i dont want to see fast linear solves replacing the current format. They should supplement eachother. My 1 euro ;-) -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...> wrote: > > ct wrote: > > > > > For me, linear FMC is about solving and nonlinear FMC is about > > > searching. > > > > > > No, I think there's more to it than just simply searching. > > Of course, but my point is that searching is a HUUUUUUGE part of > nonlinear FMC. > > > For > > example, many of us could search forever and we still would not find > > the same solutions that Per does. From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 03 Jun 2007 15:02:08 -0000

Per Kristen Fredlund wrote:

> Ok ok you didn't say "random searches". My main point anyway was
> that i think linear fast solving involves much more luck than slow
> iterative solving.

Are you taking into account that linear FMC will use averages? If you
take that into account I no longer think it is "much more".

Also, this does not take away from the fact that iterative solving
allows you to substitute luck for skill. And in /that/ sense,
iterative solving allows the solver to "use" luck much more than in
linear solving.


--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




99. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Jun 2007 13:58:00 -0000

ct wrote:

> When combining the speed aspect and the fewest moves aspect, as
> with a linear 5-minute fewest moves, I feel I am not getting the
> full speeding experience, nor am I getting the full fewest moves
> experience.

What if I removed the time limit of 5 minutes?

The focus of linear fewest moves is not to solve quickly, not at all.
The goal is simply to challenge you to solve without the safety net of
second chances.

I just find it hard to believe that the extra time would be useful
when restricted to one chance.

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




100. Re: Suggestion for a new kind of events
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Jun 2007 17:08:47 -0000

ct wrote:

> Without time limits, you could possibly do some limited mental
> backtracking, turning it into another thing completely, so I think
> time limits are necessary to keep it the sort of thing you have in
> mind

By the way, I already use premoves and the insertion technique in
linear solving. Technically, this involves "forward tracking" because
I must look into the future to see how things might be so that I know
what to expect when I get there. Backtracking means that you are going
to edit the past.

I think setting different time limits just allows for different
amounts of analysis, but this is an orthogonal issue and doesn't
change the fact that we are still doing linear solving with different
time limits.

We agreed before that 5 minutes was plenty of time, and so it
certainly didn't feel to us like this was "diluted by speed". The
purpose of the time limit was just to create fairness. We don't really
want a one week time limit because that means that some people who
have more free time available will have an advantage over people who
have less free time. 5 minutes was both enough, and not too much.

Or at least, that was the consensus back then. If you think it is time
to reassess that figure, by how much do you think we should increase
the time limit so that you won't feel it is diluted by speed?

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/

102. Linear FMC competition has started
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 11 Jun 2007 04:50:34 -0000

Here is the announcement on the main list:

http://games.groups.yahoo.com/group/speedsolvingrubikscube/message/36321

I decided to make the format just one cube each week, with a time
limit of 1 hour to solve. This time, the notated solution must also be
submitted.

Although the format is not an average, the point system will still
reward you well for coming second, and the real winner is the person
who has accumulated the most points after 12 weeks and so I still
think this works out in terms of fairness.

The advantage of this is that people who are short on time (like
myself) will still be able to participate. If I had to do an average
of 5 each week, I'd probably not have enough time to do it.

At the end of 12 weeks we'll be able to look back on it and see if the
format is any good.

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




103. Eric Johnsons fmc #158
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 17 Jun 2007 21:33:25 -0000

Hi Eric!

I studied your solution to fmc #158.

Scramble was:
R F R' D' B' U2 R2 F D L F D' B' F' R2 B' F' U' D F' B' U2 F B L'

You start off nicely with:
L' U B {D2 U2} B' F2 R D' B' D2 B2 R2 B' R D' R F2
to complete f2l.

I found a slightly better to finish the LL than your:
U' B' D B U B' D' B' D' R' D B2 U' L U' L' U2 R'

I did like so:
First do F D F' U F D' F' U' to orient corners while also solving one.
Then do the following block 3-cycle:
U' D' R2 U R U' R2 D R' U
2 moves cancel for a total of 34 (2 better than yours!)

Kind regards,

-Per

asleep at the end of each round:-P )

Ryan, I would say that you probably only use pseudoblocks which is
*almost* the same as premoves. Premoves are used to remove the
pseudoness and ease the lookahead for the rest of the solve. Making
the actual premoves to the scramble would be disallowed for your
linear challenge right? (On a real cube, not a simulator, it could
only be achieved by physical turns.)

106. Cost of inserting corner 5-cycle ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 28 Jun 2007 16:08:39 -0000

At the recent US Open I found 23 moves to solve everything but a 5-
cycle of corners in about 20-30 minutes. Then I wasted a lot of time
instead of searching for good insertions for the 5-cycle (I would've
tried two 3-cycles) and was barely able to insert two 8-move 3-cycles
before running out of time.

Question: How many moves would you expect to need to insert a 5-cycle
of corners as two 3-cycles into a 23 move solution when you have 30-
40 minutes left?

Cheers!
Stefan





107. Re: Cost of inserting corner 5-cycle ?
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 28 Jun 2007 18:51:50 -0000

Hi :-) On average i'd say one might cancel about 5 moves with 2 corner 3-cycle insertions. It does depend on the length of the "skeleton" that you are inserting them into. A skeleton of 23 sounds good. With more half turns in the skeleton it becomes harder to find good insertions. Likewise with limited time of course ;-) 30 minutes is more than i normally need anyway. I would normally sticker all those 5 corners and do the best insertion first amongst the 5 candidates: (1,2,3),(2,3,4),(3,4,5), (1,4,5) and (1,2,5). There's no guarantee that best-first insertion gives best overall ... -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...> wrote: > > At the recent US Open I found 23 moves to solve everything but a 5- > cycle of corners in about 20-30 minutes. Then I wasted a lot of time > instead of searching for good insertions for the 5-cycle (I would've > tried two 3-cycles) and was barely able to insert two 8-move 3-cycles > before running out of time. > > Question: How many moves would you expect to need to insert a 5-cycle > of corners as two 3-cycles into a 23 move solution when you have 30- > 40 minutes left? > > Cheers! > Stefan >

115. Re: Linear FM, round#5.
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 17 Jul 2007 08:54:05 -0000

Hi :-) Yes that's the thing about linear solving, you are caught by your early choices. Since the cost of undoing/changing a turn is high im not doing that. That's also why i argued before that single linear solves are almost worthless. Too much luck. And that's also why, i believe, that the linear fewest moves will be best of 10 rounds. That eliminates most of the luck. Before the competition i also decided NOT to go for my usual solution styles i do with FMC simply because they are a lot slower, and the approaches may lead to dead ends. I'm using a more flexible but less optimal approach for this challenge. I'm not really too keen on using pseudoblocks unless it's some kind of last resort. For normal fewest moves pseudoblocks are easy to deal with using scramble premoves. No such thing allowed here. But if an amazing 2x2x3 pseudoblock comes up i MAY want to use it ;-) Best wishes, Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...> wrote: > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Per Kristen Fredlund" > <aspiring_to_love@> wrote: > > > > Hi ;-) > > > > The results of the fifth round of linear fewest moves are just out. > Yet > > again some amazing solutions. 117. Re: Linear FM, round#5.
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 17 Jul 2007 10:58:26 -0000

Per Kristen Fredlund wrote:

> Yes that's the thing about linear solving, you are caught by your
> early choices. Since the cost of undoing/changing a turn is high im
> not doing that. That's also why i argued before that single linear
> solves are almost worthless. Too much luck. And that's also why, i
> believe, that the linear fewest moves will be best of 10 rounds.
> That eliminates most of the luck.

Yes, although I wonder if we will reconsider this in 20 years... Luck
is only a byproduct of our limited knowledge. For example, someone
might have a strategy that finds very good solutions in 10% of cases.
When a situation falls within that 10%, we say he is lucky. In FMC,
you can use a strategy of just trying again (about 10 times) until you
get lucky.

In 20 years, our knowledge should have increased. Will we be able to
deal with all cases -- the good, the bad, the ugly -- with equal skill
(roughly speaking) and come up with a very good solution no matter
what the case? I hope so, and that (to me) is what it means to master
the cube. In 20 years, hopefully we will observe more, be aware of
more things, and make better decisions on each move.

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/




118. Re: Linear FM, round#5.
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 17 Jul 2007 11:58:02 -0000

Hi :-) Likewise, by observing more in chess. Is it feasible that most future chess players will reach todays grandmaster level when analying the game and make decision for next moves? I don't think so. There's more to it than just brute force combinatorics. There's also positional play. This analogy may seem far fetched. What im trying to say is that it's really hard to eliminate luck by "looking further". The combinations are buidling up exponentially. So i'd say the best strategy is to choose a good route with plentitude of options and not being too greedy initially. Hmm, seems like words are knots for what i'm really trying to say. >> solves are almost worthless. Too much luck. And that's also why, i
>> believe, that the linear fewest moves will be best of 10 rounds.
>> That eliminates most of the luck.
>
> Yes, although I wonder if we will reconsider this in 20 years... Luck
> is only a byproduct of our limited knowledge. For example, someone
> might have a strategy that finds very good solutions in 10% of cases.
> When a situation falls within that 10%, we say he is lucky. In FMC,
> you can use a strategy of just trying again (about 10 times) until you
> get lucky.
>
> In 20 years, our knowledge should have increased. Will we be able to
> deal with all cases -- the good, the bad, the ugly -- with equal skill
> (roughly speaking) and come up with a very good solution no matter
> what the case? I hope so, and that (to me) is what it means to master
> the cube. In 20 years, hopefully we will observe more, be aware of
> more things, and make better decisions on each move.
>
> --
> Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/
>
>
>
>
> Yahoo! Groups Links
>
>
>

I don't think 3 trials will be enough. Based on the results so far, it
seems just as likely for the same person to get 42,45,41 as 33,39,35.

Like Per said, we need a much longer set of trials. The way I get
around the length/time problem is by doing a running tally over 12
weeks, which I think is kind of fun. So, each week, there is a winner
for that particular challenge, but the ultimate winner is the person
who has accumulated the most points over 12 weeks. Right now, I'm on
Per's tail and am trying to over take him :-) Interestingly, Per has
never won an individual challenge, but is still in the lead in the
points tally because he has performed consistently well each week.

Scramble: F' R2 D U F2 R2 D2 L R D R2 F D2 U R2 F L2 R F' U2 L' R' F R
D U2 F2 L' F' D2 U' R2 B2 R2 F U' F D2 U F L D2 U' L2 B' (45 moves)

x' y2 B' L B' U' R' B' L2 F2
And than I continued with finishing the cross and another pair in 4
more moves:
x' y2 U2 F U F

That means that in 12 moves F2L is done except for 1 pair! (pretty good
for a keyhole solver right )

I needed 47 moves in total to finish with a 3 look last layer which is
just ridiculously bad

126. Re: Czech Open challenge
From: "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 19 Jul 2007 08:27:07 -0000

Hi Arnaud =) First thing, you need to learn more algs to solve simple LL-cases like all three cycles for corners and all for edges. Corners are pretty easy to do as Ryan does it using commutators, take a look at his page to learn. For the edges there are not many cases, you can solve those in only three algs if you ignore mirrors and reverses. U-PLL (3-cycle edges) you know how to solve I assume =) Then: M' U M U2 M' U M + mirror (or reverse) And: (x') M' U' R U M U' R' U + mirror + reverse If there are simple LL-cases like two corners and two edges need to change place and sometimes orient, the rest is done, those you often can solve using a PLL after some set up turns. I will now try your first 12 moves and see what I can do to the end. // Kenneth --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "arnaudvangalen" <avgalen@...> wrote: > > Hi everyone, > > I am a total beginner at Fewest Moves, but I really like it. 132. Re: Czech Open challenge
From: "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 19 Jul 2007 21:56:34 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...> wrote: > > Then: M' U M U2 M' U M + mirror (or reverse) > Hehe, that's the same alg anyway, it is U or U' in both cases, stupid me! :P > I will now try your first 12 moves and see what I can do to the end. And that took the whole day =) Na, I actually first tried your 8 + 4 turns but got no better result than you from there and then I had other things to do. Now, later I tried again and gave it some little thinking at first: It is when you solve that third pair you get into trouble, it leaves a bad pair and all LL-edges unoriented and that's not good, at least not from my point of view. So, I go from your first eight turns instead, my first choise, linear solving: XXcross: (x' y2) U2 F2 Then I build the last pair before I down anything using: U' R U' R' Now I use "FRFR" do down both pairs and also orient LL-edges, one pair- two edges at the time: First: U' F R' F' R Second: U' F' L F (L') (I newer do that last L') This makes F2L + LL-edges oriented in 23 HTM and that's pretty good. Now, for LL I first do COLL: L2 U' L U' L' U' R U' L U (R') (agin not doing the last turn = R'. The alg is Sune + Antisune saving some turns between algs, wery nice =) This gives 33 HTM and a 1:12 chance to skip edges permutation, but it did not :( Left is a U-PLL: R d R L' F2 R' L d L2 42 HTM, 40 STM Then I found I can also solve the COLL using the mirror alg (I normaly do L because I'm left handed, using the mirror I can save that first L2 I used in the first choise, stupid me again =) This will also increase the chance to skip edge permutation to the double = 1:6 and that's a good chance: Mirror COLL (from turn 23): U L' U L U R' U' (R) 32 HTM, one turn better than before =) But again it did not skip, it gave a Z-PLL intead. So I save that last R and starts the alg from there: L' R2 D' R L' F2 B2 R L' D2 L2 R2 D' L2 R2 47 HTM, 40 STM Blah! or well, the STM is the same =) Now, i I continue to search for a better result I would try to down one of the pairs usin "FRFR" and the second (or first) using "RUR" to see if if I get a better LL from there. More possibilitys I can get if I change "RUR" to "RU2R". some of the ways may be really good, if I only can find it in time... Hope that description of my way** can give you some idéas for a approach for better results =) ** I always do linear and try diffrent ways when I do FMC. If I only get a single chance as in Ryans competition, then I use far more time to look ahead and sometimes traces the pices 5-6 turns. Then I do one turn and start to look for more posibillitys, tracing pieces again. It is more like chess than the normal twist and pray for a skip you can use if you can get back to some point and try again :P // Kenneth

140. Re: Czech Open challenge

Hi :-) Personally i prefer to stay with 25-turn scrambles due to the fact that setting up a longer scramble takes more time and is more prone to errors, like Lars said. Also i dont see the problem in having the occasional easy scramble. All have the same scramble anyway. Knowing the scramble will be "hard" may put off some from participating in the fewest moves i guess. My vote goes for no changes wrt scramble length. But if there is a majority wanting longer scrambles i still will them with repetiting parts, which makes them faster to set up, unless you are Stefan :-P Also, for fewest moves events there should always be a diagram showing the scramble or a cube showing the correct scrambled state passed around for verification purposes. In Paris there was no way to verify we had actually set up the scramble correctly, which is bad. -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Johannes Laire" <johannes.laire@...> wrote: > > Lars Petrus wrote: > > There was some research a few months ago showing that 25 move is far > > from a random scramble. 141. Fewest moves 2-edge orientation.
From: "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 23 Jul 2007 08:21:37 -0000

Think of a pretty nice solve, few turns where you manage to solve all
but two unoriented edges (adjoining, not opposite).

But those edges are not fun, many turns to solve...

I think I have the best = shortest alg for those:

L F R' F' L' U2' R U R U' R2' U2' R

13 HTM but more intresting, only 16 QTM! (I know others that can
solve the case in 12 STM but those are terrible in HTM and QTM)

Note that the end is done exactly as the "pi-OLL" using fast two-gen
turns.

Anyone got a better one?

// Kenneth

BTW: you can also do the same thing to the edges and a 3-cycle
corners simultainously by skipping those two L-turns, like this:

F R' F' U2' R U R U' R2' U2' R

Wery nice to know, bad thing is that there is not room for algs
solving complex cases like that one in the memory =)

Hello Arnaud, Your first 8 moves where on world record schedule! That 3th pair could be inserted in many ways using some extra moves but better LL. So I found a nice continuation after your 9th move: F' R' F' R2 U' R' then U2 F U' F' finishes F2L with an 11-move last layer but this alg starts with F U2 F' U' F2 R' F' R2 U' R' F' U cancelling out 3 moves at the beginning. This resulted in a 28 move solve which equals the world record! Getting this 8+4 move beginning was definately not just luck. Somehow I saw the scramble and decided to begin with the blue-yellow part (scramble was done competition style green-white) because I was sure that I could make it work out. I think the most important reason for giving a 45 move scramble is to make it easier for the judges to determine if the solution is not related to the scramble. Obviously a 34 moves solution is not related to a 45 move scramble. However if they would give a 21 move scramble (cube explorer 2-phase) or an 18 move scramble (optimal) than a 34 moves solution might just be a smart variation of (R2U2)*3 + inverse scramble + (R2U2)*3. For an untrained judge this would seem unrelated. Not influenced by me, Fewest Moves finally got the required 10 votes (http://www.speedsolving.com/showthread.php?t=842) and will now be included in the weekly competition at http://rubiks.has.it (or http://www.speedsolving.com). You can find this weeks competition at http://www.speedsolving.com/showthread.php?t=1202 and all new competitions will become available at http://www.speedsolving.com/forumdisplay.php?f=17. 151. Re: Czech Open challenge
From: "Ryan Heise" <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jul 2007 01:12:24 -0000

arnaudvangalen wrote:

> Ryan, am I correct in the following analysis?

x' y2 B' L B' U' R' B' L2 F2 x' y2 U2 F U F -
U2 L2 B L B' U L U2 R L' U' L U R' U2 B D' B' U' B D B'

Here is my analysis:

U2 L2 B L B' orients the edges according to

http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/match_squares_and_orient_edges.html

This also aligns a pair, and I join it with

U

Then finish off this step with

L

U2 R L' U' L U R' solves the edges and two corners according to

http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/five_edges_and_two_corners.html

I used the advanced approach which assumes one existing c/e pair.

U2 B D' B' U' B D B' solves the last 3 corners according to

http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/last_three_corners.html

--
Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/

153. Re: Fewest moves 2-edge orientation.
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jul 2007 07:47:13 -0000

Hi Kenneth :-) Actually you get that for only 4 additional turns ... R' and R both cancel. The 2 edges to be flipped has to be in 2 of the 3 slots used by that 3-cycle. 3 different faces may start the 3-cycle, so chances are quite high u will indeed get a killer first insertion. Cancelling 3 is not totally uncommon i guess. There's also lots of ways to do that 13-turn straight double flip. Mirrored and backwards give u 4 straight away. Then u can try cyclical shifts also ;-) ACube found a bunch ;-) U F U F2 D' L' U' L U D F2 U2 F' . (16q, 13f, 13s) U F D R2 U' R' U' R U2 R2 U' D' F' . (16q, 13f, 13s) U F' U2 F2 U D R U' R' D' F2 U F . (16q, 13f, 13s) U F' U' D' L2 U2 L U' L' U' L2 D F . (16q, 13f, 13s) U' R U D B2 U2 B' U B U B2 D' R' . (16q, 13f, 13s) U' R U2 R2 U' D' F' U F D R2 U' R' . (16q, 13f, 13s) U' R' U' R2 D B U B' U' D' R2 U2 R . (16q, 13f, 13s) U' R' D' F2 U F U F' U2 F2 U D R . (16q, 13f, 13s) F U D R2 U2 R' U R U R2 D' F' U' . (16q, 13f, 13s) F U F' U' B' R2 F R F R' F2 R2 B . (16q, 13f, 13s) F U2 F2 U' D' L' U L D F2 U' F' U' . (16q, 13f, 13s) F U2 F2 U' F U F U2 B' R' F' R B . (16q, 13f, 13s) F B U F' U' B' R2 F R F R' F2 R2 . (16q, 13f, 13s) F B U2 F2 U' F U F U2 B' R' F' R . 155. Re: Czech Open challenge
From: "guusrs" <guusrs@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 26 Jul 2007 09:50:40 -0000

Hi Arnaud, The 28 moves solution was not lucky, at least I would not call so. Let me explain. I tried 20 ways or so to insert the F2L 3rd pair and choose the best one of it. And while choosing the best option I'm not looking for a nice 2-step LL solve but actually I go for the 1-step LL solve or as an alternative for one short LL-process leaving a 3-cycle on corners or edges for inserting (using stickers) But this 3rd-pair solve was the best because after the easy 4th pair was inserted I recognized an 11 move LL alg as a conjugation of a 8 move LL alg: F' U' F2 R' F' R2 U' R'. Was it coincidence I "recognized" the 8 move LL alg? I would say no, currently I'm memorizing all 6,7,8,9 and 10 move LL algs, which means what it does to all LL corners & edges. I hope to finish this before the worldchampionships in Budapest. But this peticular 8-move alg was already in my memory sinces the 80s. See ya ;) Gus --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "arnaudvangalen" <avgalen@...> wrote: > > Hi again everyone, > > I finally found the time to try and understand your solutions. > > Ryan, am I correct in the following analysis? > "First 12 moves, than [...] U2 L2 B L B' U L U2 R L' U' L U R' U2 B > D' B' U' B D B'" > U2 L2 B L B' U L = setup top layer edges > U2 R L' U' L U R' U = finish F2L (on the right) and ELL > U B D' B' U' B D B'= COLL > > Kenneth, thanks for the detailed analysis, to bad it didn't lead to > anything good. > > Guus, I can follow the 6 moves after my first 9 (F' R' F' R2 U' R') > that finish the 3rd pair, while forming the 4th pair. But was > inserting that last pair in 4 moves while also performing PLL just a > lot of luck? And did you just happen to know the OLL for that case > that didn't change the permutation of any pieces or did you come up > with that on-the-fly? After that first 9 + 6 moves, your solution is > actually optimal U2 F U' F' F U2 F' U' F2 R' F' R2 U' R' F' U (16) = > U2 F U F' U' F2 R' F' R2 U' R' F' U (13). > > I did some optimal analysis on this solve > Directly after the scramble: 18 moves was optimal > After my first 8 moves: 15 moves was optimal > After my first 12 moves: 15 moves was optimal > After Guus' first 9 moves: 16 was optimal > After Guus' first 15 moves: 13 was optimal (and he found it!) > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Ryan Heise" <ryan@> > wrote: > > > > arnaudvangalen wrote: > > > > > Scramble: F' R2 D U F2 R2 D2 L R D R2 F D2 U R2 F L2 R F' U2 L' > R' F R > > > D U2 F2 L' F' D2 U' R2 B2 R2 F U' F D2 U F L D2 U' L2 B' (45 > moves) > > > > > > I found a great beginning of building a 2x2x3 block in 8 moves: > > > x' y2 B' L B' U' R' B' L2 F2 > > > And than I continued with finishing the cross and another pair in > 4 > > > more moves: > > > x' y2 U2 F U F > > > > [...] U2 L2 B L B' U L U2 R L' U' L U R' U2 B D' B' U' B D B' > > > > -- > > Ryan Heise http://www.ryanheise.com/cube/ > > >

time limit) is probably a good idea. But I've never done it in

You can use 3 cubes.

some (bad) spare stickers with me, you can use them if you want.

stickers for finding insertions. I guess these will be allowed if I
put them to one of the cubes.

161. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] FO 2007 FMC
From: "Arnaud van Galen" <avgalen@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Mon, 13 Aug 2007 10:38:44 +0200

Very important tip: You have exactly 1 hour, so make sure you have your solution written down clearly and checked/double-checked/tripple-checked it!

Other info and tips:
1) You can use 3 cubes (bring them yourself)
2) You can use stickers (bring them yourself). There are not really any rules about how you use them.
3) Make a global estimation for a time-schedule and try to stick with it.
4) If you have a good beginning, but can't find a good continuation you should probably try to find another beginning.

I am still really new to Fewest Moves and have only done it 5 times now. My best so far is 31 moves (http://speedsolving.com/showthread.php?t=1263)

Have fun and good luck,
Arnaud

175. Re: I do not know the history
From: "Johannes Laire" <johannes.laire@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 28 Aug 2007 10:59:50 -0000

Arnaud van Galen wrote:
>
> Fewest Moves is actually a 45 moves scramble.

It's 25 according to the regulations.

The best way to scramble would be to generate a random position and
then find a move sequence to get into that position. This has been
suggested before. But for some reason WCA seems to like the number 25,
even though it's not enough to give a fair scramble.

--
Johannes Laire





176. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: I do not know the history
From: avgalen@... <avgalen@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 28 Aug 2007 14:32:24 +0200



Johannes is correct.

According to the official rules:

http://www.worldcubeassociation.org/regulations/#fewestmovessolving
[1]
E1) Regulations are as described in Article A (Speed Solving).

http://www.worldcubeassociation.org/regulations/#speedsolving [2]
A2b) A scrambler scrambles the puzzle according to the regulations
in Article 4

http://www.worldcubeassociation.org/regulations/#scrambling [3]
4f) The number of moves to scramble a puzzle must be: 3x3x3 Cube 25
moves

However, at the US Open 2007 we got a 45 moves scramble (with
repeating parts like (R D' R' D)*3 in it) and at the Czech Open 2007
we just got a regular 45 moves scramble.

Johannes is also right about the best way to generate a good
scramble: Create a scramble with many moves (45 was found to be
sufficient, but there isn't really a reason not to use hundreds) and
optimize that to produce the same scramble in as few moves as
possible.
I use that way of generating scrambles in the weekly
competition at
speedsolving.com (http://www.speedsolving.com/showthread.php?t=1373
[4])

R2 F D U' R U2 L R2 D' U R2 D2 B' F' D R' U' L U' B F' U2 L R2 D' B'
F D' L R2 B' U F' U2 B L R U F' L R' U B' F2 U (45 moves original)
D L F2 U' L2 F2 L F' U' R2 U2 L D2 U' F L D U2 (18 moves optimal
solution, easier to apply multiple times)
On Tue, 28 Aug 2007 10:59:50 -0000, "Johannes Laire" wrote:

>

It's 25 according to the regulations.

25,
even though it's not enough to give a fair scramble.
--
Johannes Laire

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, avgalen@... <avgalen@...> wrote: > However, at the US Open 2007 we got a 45 moves scramble (with > repeating parts like (R D' R' D)*3 in it) and at the Czech Open 2007 > we just got a regular 45 moves scramble. 179. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: I do not know the history
From: avgalen@... <avgalen@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 28 Aug 2007 17:19:52 +0200



[disclaimer]The following is a summary that is meant to be easily
understanable. It is not meant to be scientifically
fullproof[/disclaimer]

If you scramble a cube, it can be restored in 18 moves on average,
somestimes less and probably always in 20 moves maximum (26/27 has
been proven)

The cube has about 43 * 10^19 possible positions and if you were to
use a 15 move scramble not all of them can be reached. Even if you
were to use 30 moves it has been argued that not all possible
positions have an equal chance to be reached. After about 45 moves
the chances of reaching any possible position was found to be "not
significantly different".

All of this doesn't mean that

1) you need to perform 45 moves to scramble it to be "not
significantly different". Optimizing a 45 moves scramble to it's 18
moves optimal scramble is considered human-friendly
2) a 19 moves optimal scramble is hard and a 17 moves optimal
scramble is easy (neither for speed or fewest moves)
3) using
20 random moves will always give you an easy scramble
4) using a 50, 100 or 1000 moves scramble would be better or worse

My recommendation would be to generate 1000 moves scrambles and to
have them sub-optimally solved to 20 moves for competitions. That
process is very fast and could be included in the official 3x3x3
scrambler by using Cube Explorers Web Interface.

My second recommendation would be to "not care" about the
theoretical quality of the scrambles anymore but focus on the
practical side:
1) Scramblers don't scramble perfectly, especially on megaminx and
bigger cubes
2) Even very long scrambles can result in very easy cases on a 2x2x2
quite often. It is almost impossible to determine what an easy case is
because people use different methods
3) Someones cube can get scrambled by a befriended scrambler and/or
judged by a befriended judge, leaving the option for fraud

I don't have any solutions for the above practical problems besides
"that's the way the cookie
crumbles" / "C'est la vie"

181. Re: I do not know the history
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 29 Aug 2007 08:51:16 -0000

There are 2 different points: - Scrambles quality. Is the cube scientifically scrambled/enough random/... ? How many random moves are necessary and can we find a better scrambling technique, like solving a random configuration? This matters for all cube events. - For fewest moves, organizers seem to prefer long scrambles, longer than the solutions found by the best competitors. Because they could supposedly cheat, and this way they want to make sure winning solutions are not inspired by the scrambling sequence. That's what I thought we were talking about in this group. Gilles. --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, avgalen@... <avgalen@...> wrote: > > > > [disclaimer]The following is a summary that is meant to be easily > understanable. It is not meant to be scientifically > fullproof[/disclaimer] > > If you scramble a cube, it can be restored in 18 moves on average, > somestimes less and probably always in 20 moves maximum (26/27 has > been proven) > > The cube has about 43 * 10^19 possible positions and if you were to > use a 15 move scramble not all of them can be reached. Even if you > were to use 30 moves it has been argued that not all possible > positions have an equal chance to be reached. After about 45 moves > the chances of reaching any possible position was found to be "not > significantly different". > > All of this doesn't mean that > > 1) you need to perform 45 moves to scramble it to be "not > significantly different". Optimizing a 45 moves scramble to it's 18 > moves optimal scramble is considered human-friendly > 2) a 19 moves optimal scramble is hard and a 17 moves optimal > scramble is easy (neither for speed or fewest moves) > 3) using > 20 random moves will always give you an easy scramble > 4) using a 50, 100 or 1000 moves scramble would be better or worse > > My recommendation would be to generate 1000 moves scrambles and to > have them sub-optimally solved to 20 moves for competitions. That > process is very fast and could be included in the official 3x3x3 > scrambler by using Cube Explorers Web Interface. > > My second recommendation would be to "not care" about the > theoretical quality of the scrambles anymore but focus on the > practical side: > 1) Scramblers don't scramble perfectly, especially on megaminx and > bigger cubes > 2) Even very long scrambles can result in very easy cases on a 2x2x2 > quite often. It is almost impossible to determine what an easy case is > because people use different methods > 3) Someones cube can get scrambled by a befriended scrambler and/or > judged by a befriended judge, leaving the option for fraud > > I don't have any solutions for the above practical problems besides > "that's the way the cookie > crumbles" / "C'est la vie" > On Tue, 28 Aug 2007 13:45:33 -0000,
